href=\"http://twitter.com/share?url=https%3A%2F%2Fcafehayek.com%2F2020%2F08%2Fsteering-the-economy-over-a-cliff.html&#38;text=Steering the Economy Over a Cliff - Cafe Hayek\" class=\"twitter-share-button\">Tweet</a></p><p>Here’s a letter to the <em>Wall Street Journal</em>:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Editor:</p>\n<p>Richard Sexton and Daniel Sumner rightly condemn efforts to prosecute farmers for so-called “price gouging” in the midst of the covid lockdowns (“<a href=\"https://www.wsj.com/articles/new-yorks-ag-lays-a-rotten-egg-11598822036?mod=opinion_lead_pos7\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">New York’s AG Lays a Rotten Egg</a>,” August 31).</p>\n<p>Prices steer goods, services, and inputs into their most economically productive uses. Therefore, for government to restrict prices in markets from moving up and down as people adjust to changing realities of the economy is just as absurd and dangerous as it would be for government to restrict steering wheels on cars from turning left and right as drivers adjust to changing realities of the road.</p>\n<p>Sincerely,<br />\nDonald J. Boudreaux<br />\nProfessor of Economics<br />\nand<br />\nMartha and Nelson Getchell Chair for the Study of Free Market Capitalism at the Mercatus Center<br />\nGeorge Mason University<br />\nFairfax, Here&#8217;s his conclusion:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Past increases in the federal minimum wage always resulted in many more people pushed into jobs paying below minimum and usually losing whatever benefits they previously enjoyed. Far from being an effective and humane way to raise the lowest incomes the unintended consequence of increasing the federal minimum wage has, in fact, been to force hundreds of thousands more Americans into substandard jobs and make the poorest workers poorer.</p></blockquote>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.hoover.org/research/china-economic-threat\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">David Henderson asks if China is an economic threat</a>.</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.washingtonexaminer.com/opinion/californias-green-dream-is-actually-a-nightmare?mkt_tok=eyJpIjoiTUdZME0yUXhaREppWXprNSIsInQiOiJYVEdIYmxXN3gxSjg3MWhyWkNBZmZnUVE1SnRNV3NEaFFSV3ZUS1pmMFNLQldTUlJDQVYzcUQ0OUJmcEFLQ1ZIWmx5TmJlRlpBU1NsWlJ6K3lDcytXZHFkdjZhYkRxclwvcUpyRmFkVUxBdkhObXM2NXNzQ2ROMzc1dEZzVHBIdEoifQ%3D%3D\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Mark Perry explains that California&#8217;s &#8220;green dream&#8221; is a nightmare</a>.</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://reason.com/2020/08/27/no-donald-trump-did-not-shrink-government/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Regardless of which of the two unambiguous evils &#8211; Biden or Trump &#8211; you regard as the lesser, understand that Trump is no champion of small government</a>.</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://marginalrevolution.com/marginalrevolution/2020/08/the-quality-adjusted-rate-of-price-inflation-is-much-higher-than-measured.html\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Tyler Cowen is correct: today&#8217;s actual rate of inflation is much higher than the official rate</a>.</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.wsj.com/articles/critical-ethnic-studies-returns-to-california-11598568504?mod=opinion_lead_pos6\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Writing in the <em>Wall Street Journal</em>, Bill Evers laments the curriculum in California&#8217;s government-operated schools.</a> A slice:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Welcome to “critical ethnic studies,” which boils down to vulgar Marxism, identity politics and victimology. class=\"twitter-share-button\">Tweet</a></p><p>Santa Clara University political scientist Peter Minowitz wrote a penetrating review essay of Ibram X. Kendi&#8217;s <em>How To Be an Antiracist</em>, and Robin DiAngelo&#8217;s <em>White Fragility: Why It’s So Hard for White People to Talk about Racism</em>. I might, if pressed, pick a tiny nit or two with this essay, but Peter&#8217;s argument is solid and clear and important and timely, and I heartily agree with his conclusion. It&#8217;s very much worth a careful read.</p>\n<p>Here&#8217;s the opening. The remainder of the essay continues beneath the fold.<br />\n&#8230;&#8230;..</p>\n<blockquote><p><strong>How to Be a Better—and Less Fragile—Antiracist</strong></p>\n<p><strong>By Peter Minowitz, Santa Clara University</strong></p>\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\">When you see something that is not right, not fair, not just, you have a moral obligation to do something, to say something, and not be quiet. –Congressman John Lewis</p>\n<p>Professors typically lament the damage President Trump has caused by exaggerating, stereotyping, and demonizing. The ones who drift into activism, however, are not immune to these discursive disorders. I shall explore this problem by scrutinizing two bestsellers: <em>How To Be an Antiracist </em>(One World, 2019) by Ibram X. Kendi and <em>White Fragility: Why It’s So Hard for White People to Talk about Racism </em>(Beacon Press, 2018) by Robin DiAngelo. The authors are already national icons, they extol each other’s work, and their books are being assigned widely within America’s campuses and businesses.</p></blockquote>\n<p> <a Nassim Taleb points out that a map is very helpful for getting around Paris. But not if the map you’re using is a map of New York. Using the wrong map unknowingly is worse than no map at all &#8211; it leads you to overconfidence that can be more harmful than confronting the reality that you’re lost.</i></p>\n<p><i>Scientists are human beings with their own limitations. 16:59:57","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 16:59:57","title":"An Ignorant and Dishonest Performance","link":"https://cafehayek.com/?p=49902","description":"(Don Boudreaux) \n<!-- Facebook Like Button Vivacity Infotech BEGIN -->\n<div class=\"fb-like\" data-href=\"http://cafehayek.com/2020/09/an-ignorant-and-dishonest-performance.html\" data-layout=\"standard\" data-action=\"like\" data-show-faces=\"false\" data-size=\"small\" data-width=\"450\" data-share=\"\" ></div>\n<!-- Facebook Like Button Vivacity Infotech END -->\n<p><a href=\"http://twitter.com/share?url=https%3A%2F%2Fcafehayek.com%2F2020%2F09%2Fan-ignorant-and-dishonest-performance.html&#38;text=An Ignorant and Dishonest Performance - Cafe Hayek\" class=\"twitter-share-button\">Tweet</a></p><p>In what must be one of <a href=\"https://twitter.com/hamandcheese/status/1301925883276668928?s=11\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">the most ignorant tweets</a> authored by someone supposedly well-informed and knowledgeable about economics and public policy in the U.S., Sam Hammond of the Niskanen Center writes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>The Ex-Im fight isn&#8217;t really about &#8220;crony capitalism.&#8221; Every industrialized nation has an export credit agency.</p>\n<p>See it for what it was and is: A demonstration project to test the efficacy of the Koch&#8217;s advocacy network at mobilizing right wing populism.</p></blockquote>\n<p>What nonsense, at once illogical and dishonest, and throughout gratuitously insulting.</p>\n<p>First comes the illogic. We are to believe that because every industrialized nation has an export-credit agency, then it&#8217;s obvious that such agencies &#8211; including the one here in the U.S. &#8211; serve the greater good rather than serve the interests of cronies. Alternatively, I suppose, Hammond might expect us to believe that if every industrialized country has cronyist institutions, there is then no good reason for anyone in the U.S. to protest our own such institutions.</p>\n<p>I need say no more for anyone of intelligence to detect the obvious weakness of either interpretation of Hammond&#8217;s claim. (For those who might still perhaps find some potential merit in Hammond&#8217;s claim, ask yourself if you believe that agricultural price supports, protective tariffs, and rent control &#8211; policies also widely practiced by many other industrialized nations &#8211; are thereby proven to be policies that promote the greater good and not likely to be motivated and maintained by cronyism.)</p>\n<p>Next comes the dishonesty. Hammond personally knows many of the people at Mercatus, and in other like-minded institutions, who write and testify against ExIm. He therefore <em>knows</em> &#8211; or recklessly refuses to know &#8211; that they sincerely believe that ExIm is an unjustified creature of cronyism. Even if they are mistaken, Hammond &#8211; who once worked with them &#8211; must know that their convictions are held sincerely rather than worn as a costume for purely political ends. Hammond&#8217;s insinuation that these opponents of ExIm do what they do, write what they write, say what they say, because they are paid to do so is despicable. He knows that these people are not mercenary. He may legitimately disagree with their economics, their values, and their policy positions &#8211; and he might even be correct to so disagree &#8211; but he cannot possibly really believe that these people are insincere mercenaries with no real convictions about the matter.</p>\n<p>Nor, by the way, does Hammond have any reason whatsoever to believe that Charles Koch himself is insincere in his opposition to cronyism or in his belief that Ex-Im is an instance of cronyism. Everything in the long history of Mr. Koch&#8217;s support for liberal causes reveals beyond any doubt that he would understand that ExIm is an economically harmful cronyist outfit. (Ditto for Mr. Koch&#8217;s employees.) No &#8211; let me make this point stronger: Hammond surely <em>knows</em> that the belief held by Mr. Koch and his employees that ExIm is a cronyist institution is a belief held sincerely. That Hammond knowingly suggests otherwise is worse than contemptible.</p>\n<p>Hammond should apologize.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?a=_hyKZDnLyPY:TB4cpnKCSbI:yIl2AUoC8zA\"><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?d=yIl2AUoC8zA\" border=\"0\"></img></a> <a 16:59:57","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 16:59:57","title":"Quotation of the Day…","link":"https://cafehayek.com/?p=49847","description":"(Don Boudreaux) \n<!-- Facebook Like Button Vivacity Infotech BEGIN -->\n<div class=\"fb-like\" data-href=\"http://cafehayek.com/2020/08/quotation-of-the-day-3267.html\" data-layout=\"standard\" data-action=\"like\" data-show-faces=\"false\" data-size=\"small\" data-width=\"450\" data-share=\"\" ></div>\n<!-- Facebook Like Button Vivacity Infotech END -->\n<p><a href=\"http://twitter.com/share?url=https%3A%2F%2Fcafehayek.com%2F2020%2F08%2Fquotation-of-the-day-3267.html&#38;text=Quotation of the Day... - Cafe Hayek\" class=\"twitter-share-button\">Tweet</a></p><p>&#8230; is from pages 39-40 of Kristian Niemietz’s superb 2019 book, <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/gp/product/B07N7HK6LV/ref=dbs_a_def_rwt_hsch_vapi_tkin_p1_i0\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"><em>Socialism: The Failed Idea That Never Dies</em></a> (original emphasis):</p>\n<blockquote><p><i><img class=\"alignleft wp-image-49848\" src=\"https://cafehayek.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/Screen-Shot-2020-08-29-at-4.17.47-PM.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"162\" height=\"98\" />It is a common misunderstanding that the main role of competition is to act as a spur: we work harder when we are under competitive pressure than we do when we can take our current position for granted. But this was never the main issue: socialist economies had other (less benign) ways of spurring people on. What they lacked, however, was the knowledge-creating capacity of competition. <strong>This</strong> is the main role of competition in economic life. Socialist economies deprive themselves of the vast amount of knowledge created by competition. To a lesser extent, so do market economies that hinder the competitive process, for example, by erecting barriers to market entry.</i></p></blockquote>\n<p><strong>DBx</strong>: Because this reality is often ignored, it must be often repeated: Any government-erected obstruction of the ways that people may invest and spend their own money &#8211; as entrepreneurs, investors, and consumers &#8211; destroys some amount of economic knowledge that would otherwise have been generated and acted upon. (Even when, for some non-economic reason, such an obstruction can be justified, some relevant economic knowledge is nevertheless destroyed.)</p>\n<p>This reality makes the claim that industrial policy will actually improve the economy highly suspect. Industrial policy <em>necessarily</em> destroys economic knowledge, and the more extensive such a policy, the more knowledge it destroys. Until and unless advocates of industrial policy do what they have yet to do, or even to attempt to do &#8211; namely, explain how government officials will generate and acquire knowledge to replace that which their trade restraints and subsidies destroy &#8211; they should be ignored.</p>\n<p>The fact that industrial-policy advocates are <em>not</em> ignored testifies only to the abysmal state of the public’s understanding of economic reality.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?a=Dw2AmL-sxuM:mbXHls_ulos:yIl2AUoC8zA\"><img href=\"http://twitter.com/share?url=https%3A%2F%2Fcafehayek.com%2F2020%2F09%2Fquotation-of-the-day-3269.html&#38;text=Quotation of the Day... - Cafe Hayek\" class=\"twitter-share-button\">Tweet</a></p><p>&#8230; is from pages 128-129 of the 1971 Augustus M. Kelley reprint of the 1880 Sixth American <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Treatise-Political-Economy-Jean-Baptise/dp/1602061912/ref=asap_bc?ie=UTF8\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">edition</a> of <a href=\"http://oll.libertyfund.org/pages/say-1767-1832-an-annotated-bibliography-by-david-hart\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Jean-Baptiste Say</a>’s 1803 <a href=\"http://www.econlib.org/library/Say/sayT.html\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"><em>A Treatise on Political Economy</em></a> (<em>Traité d’économie politique</em>):</p>\n<blockquote><p><i><img class=\"alignleft wp-image-49867\" src=\"https://cafehayek.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/Unknown-1.jpeg\" alt=\"\" width=\"117\" height=\"149\" />There are some truths so completely self-evident, that demonstration is quite superfluous. This is one of that number. For who will attempt to deny, that the certainty of enjoying the fruits of one’s land, capital, and labour, is the most powerful inducement to render them productive? Or who is dull enough to doubt, that no one knows so well as the proprietor how to make the best use of his property?</i></p></blockquote>\n<p><strong>DBx</strong>: Sadly, the world today is filled with people who are indeed so dull &#8211; so dull despite, in many cases, boasting terminal degrees in subjects such as economics, philosophy, and law from universities sporting names such as M.I.T., Columbia, and Harvard.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?a=OKedN9UvlnU:IemL8FhxjRE:yIl2AUoC8zA\"><img -->\n<div class=\"fb-like\" data-href=\"http://cafehayek.com/2020/08/quotation-of-the-day-3266.html\" data-layout=\"standard\" data-action=\"like\" data-show-faces=\"false\" data-size=\"small\" data-width=\"450\" data-share=\"\" ></div>\n<!-- Facebook Like Button Vivacity Infotech END -->\n<p><a href=\"http://twitter.com/share?url=https%3A%2F%2Fcafehayek.com%2F2020%2F08%2Fquotation-of-the-day-3266.html&#38;text=Quotation of the Day... - Cafe Hayek\" class=\"twitter-share-button\">Tweet</a></p><p>&#8230; is from page 87 of economist Diane Coyle’s 2014 book, <a href=\"http://www.amazon.com/GDP-Brief-but-Affectionate-History/dp/0691156794/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&#38;ie=UTF8&#38;qid=1392641468&#38;sr=1-1\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"><em>GDP</em></a> (footnote deleted; link added):</p>\n<blockquote><p><i><img class=\"alignleft wp-image-49845\" src=\"https://cafehayek.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/41tRz8QKsEL-1._SX325_BO1204203200_-197x300.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"142\" height=\"217\" srcset=\"https://cafehayek.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/41tRz8QKsEL-1._SX325_BO1204203200_-197x300.jpg 197w, https://cafehayek.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/41tRz8QKsEL-1._SX325_BO1204203200_.jpg 327w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 142px) 100vw, 142px\" />It has been rather fashionable to claim that there is &#8220;too much&#8221; choice. The evidence points the other way. <a href=\"https://www.nber.org/chapters/c6068.pdf\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Econometric estimates</a> of the value consumers derive from additional varieties of certain products, say of cereals, or book titles, indicate that this is large even for seemingly trivial innovations like apple cinnamon flavor Cheerios. What must be the benefit when adding together everything from minor innovations in cereals, toothpastes, and flavors of tea, through more obviously novel innovations such as Velcro fastening or hybrid cars, to those we normally think of as high-tech, like smartphone and tablet computers, genetically targeted pharmaceuticals, and new materials such as graphene?</i></p></blockquote>\n<p><strong>DBx</strong>: No serious person with normal human sensibilities believes that achieving ever-greater access to material goods and services is or ought to be the be-all and end-all of human existence. Non-material, &#8216;higher,’ experiences &#8211; love, friendship, a sense of purpose, dignity, spirituality, self-respect &#8211; are of course deeply important.</p>\n<p>And while sometimes the pursuit of the material comes at the expense of the &#8216;higher,’ this trade-off isn’t always or even typically required. Very often acquiring greater access to material goods and services better enables the achievement of the &#8216;higher.’ Visiting grandma is more satisfying if she’s alive, and she might well be alive because of some pharmaceutical products or because modern electronic communications devices gave her sufficiently early warning to evacuate before Hurricane Wallop came ashore.</p>\n<p>It’s easy and oh-so-cool for people awash in modernity’s material amenities to parade their ethical sophistication by denouncing what they take to be the excessive, even animalistic, &#8220;addiction&#8221; (as they often call it) of other human beings to material goods and services. (Often such denunciations feature as examples devices that are used chiefly for entertainment, such as flatscreen TVs &#8211; as if the opportunity for escape, to be entertained, is a somewhat contemptible human desire.) But such denunciations are made by people who cast a too-shallow glance at their fellow human beings.</p>\n<p>Sure, the Joneses seem rather too materialistic, what with mom and dad snacking on microwave popcorn as they watch &#8220;Schitt&#8217;s Creek&#8221; on their flatscreen TV, while each of the junior Joneses sits alone in his or her room texting friends or surfing the Internet. The Joneses aren’t doing what some intellectuals fancy they should be doing. They’re not at the bowling alley; they’re not sitting on the porch talking with the neighbors; they’re not at a townhall meeting; they’re doing nothing found in a Norman Rockwell painting.</p>\n<p>Yet what are the other experiences that the Joneses enjoy because of their ready access to an abundance of material goods and services? Well, if they are typical Americans they’re healthier than they would have been even just a few decades ago because of the availability of some drugs and medical devices and procedures that only relatively recently became available. The Joneses can talk daily, without worrying about the cost, in real time face-to-face with grandma and grandpa who live hundreds of miles away. When the Joneses travel by automobile they’re less likely to be killed or seriously injured than were their parents at their age. They can acquire recorded music and many books within seconds &#8211; and thus, if they wish, elevate their tastes and improve their minds.</p>\n<p>And they can afford to give to charitable causes, send $250 by Venmo to a struggling nephew, and volunteer on Sundays to work at a community theater &#8211; activities invisible to those who only see the Joneses perched in front of a flatscreen TV watching a silly comedy show.</p>\n<p>Of course not everyone spends his or her time and material prosperity well. Some people are truly shallow and hyper-materialistic by any standard. But it’s untrue that greater material abundance is necessarily &#8211; or even, in practice, usually &#8211; bought at the expense of the deeper and better and higher aspects of what it means to be fully human.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?a=b4jDs_XO_UI:qJveRWA60Ro:yIl2AUoC8zA\"><img data-share=\"\" ></div>\n<!-- Facebook Like Button Vivacity Infotech END -->\n<p><a href=\"http://twitter.com/share?url=https%3A%2F%2Fcafehayek.com%2F2020%2F09%2Fsome-links-1631.html&#38;text=Some Links - Cafe Hayek\" class=\"twitter-share-button\">Tweet</a></p><p><a href=\"https://www.creators.com/read/veronique-de-rugy/09/20/theres-no-such-thing-as-a-free-tax-holiday\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">My intrepid Mercatus Center colleague Veronique de Rugy isn&#8217;t impressed by Trump&#8217;s proposal to give many Americans a &#8216;holiday&#8217; from paying payroll taxes</a>. A slice:</p>\n<blockquote><p>I understand that people don&#8217;t like to pay the payroll tax. But it should go without saying that if you stop paying the tax that funds the Social Security system, you should pair it with an equivalent reduction in benefits. If that doesn&#8217;t happen, then the benefits for Social Security will have to come from the general funds, which is what happened during past payroll tax holidays. That means that the federal government will have to borrow the money and expect future generations to pay the bill plus interest.</p></blockquote>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.aier.org/article/plato-v-mises-mises-wins/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Mike Munger chalks up one in the win column for Mises</a>.</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.aier.org/article/walkaway-and-the-civility-gap/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">My colleague Dan Klein writes about the WalkAway movement</a>. A slice:</p>\n<blockquote><p>One way to think about it is that because leftists have fallen into terribly wrongheaded beliefs, and become committed to them, they cannot tolerate civil discourse and civil social interaction: They have grown fragile, and as they replicate themselves in academia and elsewhere, increasingly more fragile all the time. Thus they rely on incivility to shield them from any stress that would expose their wrongheadedness.</p>\n<p>Another way to think about it is that they choose to believe leftist ideas, and that choice is itself socially irresponsible and unbecoming. Adam Smith taught that <a href=\"https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=2930837\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">justice is trilayered</a>, and the most extensive layer is estimating objects properly.</p></blockquote>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.wsj.com/articles/one-step-ahead-of-the-woke-mob-11598637380?mod=searchresults&#38;page=1&#38;pos=1\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">In the <em>Wall Street Journal</em> Dan Akst profiles the wonderful writer Lionel Shriver.</a> A slice:</p>\n<blockquote><p>“We’ve never before responded to a contagion by closing down whole countries,” she observes. The pandemic and lockdown created fear, isolation and a holiday from normal rules, which built a pyre: “The far left’s consuming obsession with race has been building for years. Then for months these same folks have been cooped up, fuming over their computers. The 2020 explosion of Black Lives Matter was enabled by Covid.” Floyd’s killing “just lit the match.”</p></blockquote>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/opinions/the-us-is-facing-many-old-european-anxieties/2020/09/01/bf06722a-ec74-11ea-ab4e-581edb849379_story.html\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">From George Will&#8217;s latest</a>:</p>\n<blockquote><p>In the current disorders, [Martin] Gurri says, mayors and governors have succumbed to “infantile panic”: Many state and local officials are liberal Democrats who share the ideals of the protesters and are “paralyzed by fear of doing anything that might transform them into villains of the narrative.”</p></blockquote>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.econlib.org/repression-of-economic-freedom-the-case-of-eggs/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Pierre Lemieux laments the economic ignorance that leads people &#8211; especially people possessing power &#8211; to support government-imposed controls on prices</a>.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a 16:59:57","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 16:59:57","title":"Politics Isn’t for Me","link":"https://cafehayek.com/?p=49865","description":"(Don Boudreaux) \n<!-- Facebook Like Button Vivacity Infotech BEGIN -->\n<div class=\"fb-like\" data-href=\"http://cafehayek.com/2020/08/politics-isnt-for-me.html\" data-layout=\"standard\" data-action=\"like\" data-show-faces=\"false\" data-size=\"small\" data-width=\"450\" data-share=\"\" ></div>\n<!-- Facebook Like Button Vivacity Infotech END -->\n<p><a href=\"http://twitter.com/share?url=https%3A%2F%2Fcafehayek.com%2F2020%2F08%2Fpolitics-isnt-for-me.html&#38;text=Politics Isn't for Me - Cafe Hayek\" class=\"twitter-share-button\">Tweet</a></p><p>Here’s my response to someone who (I’m proud to report) “majorly enjoy[s] Cafe Hayek”:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Mr. Lawrence:</p>\n<p>Thanks for your e-mail. I’m delighted that you like Café Hayek. And I’m touched that you urge me to “run for Congress to put [my] ideas into effect.”</p>\n<p>Alas, I’m among the last people on earth to have any interest in seeking political office. The reason is more than that I would find campaigning to be intolerably tedious and shameful. The foundational reason is that I have negative interest in holding such office. If a guaranteed seat in Congress were offered to me without my having to lift a finger to win it, I’d turn it down flat and immediately. I am ethically and intellectually unfit to exercise even as much as 1/535<sup>th</sup> of a quantum of power over others. In addition, were I to possess political power I’d be tempted to sell my soul. And being human, I’m too likely to succumb to such temptation. Better to avoid it.</p>\n<p>Even if nothing above is a good reason to avoid politics, I find justification in this insight from Lord Acton:</p>\n<p style=\"padding-left: 40px;\"><em>Institutions and laws have their roots not in the ingenuity of statesmen, but as much as possible in the opinion of the people.</em>*</p>\n<p>I have the good fortune not only to be a college professor, but also to <em>love</em> teaching. I teach economics. I give talks on economics. I write on economics. Nothing that I do during my earthly tenure is apt to have any impact on policy outcomes. But if I were to have any impact at all, it would only be through helping to shape the opinion of the people. Ultimately, we are governed exclusively by that.</p>\n<p>Sincerely,<br />\nDon</p>\n<p>* Lord Acton, <i>Essays in Religion, Politics, and Morality</i> (Indianapolis: Liberty Fund, 1988), page 569. (I can find no date for the quoted passage.)</p></blockquote>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?a=RDCg80M97Po:w9k2432wP3A:yIl2AUoC8zA\"><img 16:59:57","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 16:59:57","title":"Oren Cass Still Misses the Unseen","link":"https://cafehayek.com/?p=49843","description":"(Don Boudreaux) \n<!-- Facebook Like Button Vivacity Infotech BEGIN -->\n<div class=\"fb-like\" data-href=\"http://cafehayek.com/2020/08/oren-cass-is-still-misses-the-unseen.html\" data-layout=\"standard\" data-action=\"like\" data-show-faces=\"false\" data-size=\"small\" data-width=\"450\" data-share=\"\" ></div>\n<!-- Facebook Like Button Vivacity Infotech END -->\n<p><a href=\"http://twitter.com/share?url=https%3A%2F%2Fcafehayek.com%2F2020%2F08%2Foren-cass-is-still-misses-the-unseen.html&#38;text=Oren Cass Still Misses the Unseen - Cafe Hayek\" class=\"twitter-share-button\">Tweet</a></p><p>Here’s a letter to the <em>New York Times</em>:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Editor:</p>\n<p>In “<a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2020/08/27/opinion/us-gdp-coronavirus.html?smid=em-share\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">The Elite Needs to Give Up Its G.D.P. Fetish</a>” (August 28) Oren Cass overstates the importance that economists attach to material goods and services as he understates the importance that workers attach to these things.</p>\n<p>No competent economist believes that humans do or should care only, or even mainly, about outputs exchanged in markets. But economists do recognize that better access to such outputs enhances individuals’ abilities to attain non-material goals. Community involvement, education, art, and the leisure necessary to care for family and friends are prime examples of non-material ends made more accessible as people’s material desires are better satisfied.</p>\n<p>Still, ordinary people obviously care more than Cass realizes about satisfying material desires. By agreeing to take pay cuts – that is, by agreeing to reduce their access to outputs exchanged in markets – workers facing competition from imports or technology could thereby keep their jobs and, in turn, retain the specific community connections that Cass says workers value so highly. Yet workers generally refuse to take pay cuts. They thus reveal that they value continued access to material goods and services more highly than they value the non-material experiences and community connections that they sacrifice by refusing to take lower pay.</p>\n<p>The protectionism that Cass proposes can, at best, protect only some Americans from having to make this trade-off. And it can do so only by compelling <em>other</em> Americans – those who pay higher prices for consumer goods and those denied the better jobs that would otherwise be created – to suffer reduced access to material goods and services.</p>\n<p>Avoiding such an injustice by keeping trade free is a non-material value that I and other classical liberals cherish and wish would receive greater respect from politicians and pundits.</p>\n<p>Sincerely,<br />\nDonald J. Boudreaux<br />\nProfessor of Economics<br />\nand<br />\nMartha and Nelson Getchell Chair for the Study of Free Market Capitalism at the Mercatus Center<br />\nGeorge Mason University<br />\nFairfax, VA 22030</p></blockquote>\n<p>&#8230;..</p>\n<p>The above letter is already too long (for a letter), yet it still ignores several other fallacies found in Cass&#8217;s essay.</p>\n<div Industrial Policy Works!","link":"https://cafehayek.com/?p=49846","description":"(Don Boudreaux) \n<!-- Facebook Like Button Vivacity Infotech BEGIN -->\n<div class=\"fb-like\" data-href=\"http://cafehayek.com/2020/08/same-worn-script-scribbled-with-fiction.html\" data-layout=\"standard\" data-action=\"like\" data-show-faces=\"false\" data-size=\"small\" data-width=\"450\" data-share=\"\" ></div>\n<!-- Facebook Like Button Vivacity Infotech END -->\n<p><a href=\"http://twitter.com/share?url=https%3A%2F%2Fcafehayek.com%2F2020%2F08%2Fsame-worn-script-scribbled-with-fiction.html&#38;text=See! No Gulags! Industrial Policy Works! - Cafe Hayek\" class=\"twitter-share-button\">Tweet</a></p><blockquote><p>Here’s a letter to the <em>Washington Post</em>:</p>\n<p>Editor:</p>\n<p>As displayed again in Jeanne Whalen’s report “<a href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/business/2020/08/26/republicans-favor-industrial-policy/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" data-auth=\"NotApplicable\">To counter China, some Republicans are abandoning free-market orthodoxy</a>” (Aug. 28), advocates of industrial policy – including Oren Cass and other conservatives – perform according to a well-rehearsed script.</p>\n<p>Act 1: Repeat fallacies as if they’re established facts. An example, as reported by Ms. Whalen, is to assert that “U.S. businesses also aren’t investing as much as they used to on new equipment.” In reality, <a href=\"https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/CAPB50001SQ\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" data-auth=\"NotApplicable\">U.S. industrial capacity</a> hit an all-time high in the first quarter of 2020, making it 16% greater than it was when China joined the World Trade Organization in 2001 and 156% greater than in 1976.</p>\n<p>Act 2: Divert attention with red herrings – such as when they note that U.S. government research and development spending has since 1976 fallen as a percentage of gross domestic product. True, but unmentioned is the fact that private businesses – which are accused of being myopically focused on enriching shareholders with cash in the short-run – have steadily <i>increased</i> R&#38;D spending. <a href=\"https://www.stlouisfed.org/on-the-economy/2018/may/rd-business-spending-up-government-spending-flat\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" data-auth=\"NotApplicable\">In 1976 business R&#38;D expenditures were 0.8% of GDP; in 2016 business R&#38;D expenditures were 1.8% of GDP</a> – thus causing <i>total</i> R&#38;D expenditures in 2016 to be 2.7% of GDP (compared to 2.1% in 1976).*</p>\n<p>Act 3: Straw-man the case against industrial policy – as when one industrial-policy fan <a href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/business/2020/08/26/republicans-favor-industrial-policy/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">is reported</a> to have accused libertarian opponents of such policy of believing it to be “equal to” Soviet-style central planning.</p>\n<p>Act 4: Steadfastly ignore the key question asked by industrial-policy opponents: “How will government officials get the information they need to allocate resources better than resources are allocated by entrepreneurs, investors, and consumers risking and spending their own money in competitive markets?” Industrial-policy proponents simply continue to believe, as a matter of faith, that government will somehow know what to do and how best to do it – a fact that adds irony to the error of the accusation made by another industrial-policy proponent that those of us who oppose industrial policy are “naïve.”</p>\n<p>Sincerely,<br />\nDonald J. Boudreaux<br />\nProfessor of Economics<br />\nand<br />\nMartha and Nelson Getchell Chair for the Study of Free Market Capitalism at the Mercatus Center<br />\nGeorge Mason University<br />\nFairfax, VA 22030</p>\n<p>* I converted all R&#38;D dollar figures in the linked piece into 2012 dollars using <a href=\"https://data.bls.gov/cgi-bin/cpicalc.pl\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">this inflation adjuster</a>, and then used <a href=\"https://www.multpl.com/us-gdp-inflation-adjusted/table/by-year\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">this link</a> as my source for annual U.S. GDP.</p></blockquote>\n<p>&#8230;..</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.cato.org/blog/conservative-industrial-policy-china-threat\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">See also Scott Lincicome&#8217;s brilliant response to this report on conservatives&#8217; embrace of industrial policy</a>.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a class=\"twitter-share-button\">Tweet</a></p><p>Here’s a letter to the <em>Wall Street Journal</em>:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Editor:</p>\n<p>Tunku Varadarajan rightly decries progressives’ criticism of the British singer Adele for wearing African-style Bantu knots (“<a href=\"https://www.wsj.com/articles/the-sun-never-sets-on-the-woke-empire-11599062724?mod=opinion_lead_pos9\" target=\"_blank\" they’re blind to the very real disrespect and danger that lurk in private-property appropriation.</p>\n<p>Sincerely,<br />\nDonald J. 16:59:57","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 16:59:57","title":"Bonus Quotation of the Day…","link":"https://cafehayek.com/?p=49873","description":"(Don Boudreaux) \n<!-- Facebook Like Button Vivacity Infotech BEGIN -->\n<div class=\"fb-like\" data-href=\"http://cafehayek.com/2020/09/bonus-quotation-of-the-day-526.html\" data-layout=\"standard\" data-action=\"like\" data-show-faces=\"false\" data-size=\"small\" data-width=\"450\" data-share=\"\" ></div>\n<!-- Facebook Like Button Vivacity Infotech END -->\n<p><a href=\"http://twitter.com/share?url=https%3A%2F%2Fcafehayek.com%2F2020%2F09%2Fbonus-quotation-of-the-day-526.html&#38;text=Bonus Quotation of the Day... - Cafe Hayek\" class=\"twitter-share-button\">Tweet</a></p><p>… is from page 84 of the May 9th, 2020, draft of the important forthcoming monograph from Deirdre McCloskey and Alberto Mingardi, <i>The Illiberal and Anti-Entrepreneurial State of Mariana Mazzucato</i>:</p>\n<blockquote><p><em><img class=\"alignleft wp-image-49874\" src=\"https://cafehayek.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/Screen-Shot-2020-09-01-at-3.55.40-PM.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"157\" height=\"144\" />Such is the reiterated premise of Mazzucato’s books: that the State courageously ventures into the High Frontier, “taking the kind of risk the private sector is not willing to take.” She does not worry overmuch that the reason no private entity will so venture is that the venture is rubbish in an economic sense—and probably in any other sense ones cares to mention—and that courageous spending on the High Frontier lowers human welfare.</em></p></blockquote>\n<p><strong>DBx</strong>: It<span class=\"None\">’s flabbergasting that </span>anyone over the age of five believes that giving Jones the power to spend Smith<span class=\"None\">’s money will result in Smith’s money being spent more wisely than if Smith spends that money. Triply flabbergasting is the fact that such a fairy tale is believed and trumpeted by someone trained as an economist.</span></p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"https://www.wsj.com/articles/commerce-and-manners-in-edmund-burkes-political-economy-review-tradition-yes-and-markets-too-11598915767?mod=opinion_reviews_pos1\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">James Grant reviews, in the <i>Wall Street Journal</i>, Gregory Collins&#8217;s new book, <i>Commerce and Manners in Edmund Burke’s Political Economy</i></a>. A slice from Grant&#8217;s review:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Liberty was the touchstone of English monetary arrangements, Burke’s argument ran. “Cash,” as gold was known, was the one and only legal tender. Of course, bank notes were handier than ingots, but nobody had to accept such IOUs (not even the Bank of England’s) in lieu of gold itself. Weighing the soundness of the issuing bank, an Englishman could choose one or the other.</p>\n<p>Not so the Frenchman. The pure paper assignat was money by law. Modern readers will scarcely take exception to such a system, as they know no other. “This note is legal tender for all debts, public and private” is the legend stamped on every dollar bill.</p>\n<p>To Burke, however, it was the very absence of coercion that crowned the English monetary regime. “They forget,” he wrote of the revolting French, “that, in England, not one shilling of paper money of any description is received but of choice; that the whole has had its origin in cash actually deposited; and that it is convertible at pleasure, in an instant and without the smallest loss, into cash again. Our paper is of value in commerce, because in law it is of none.”</p></blockquote>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a Here&#8217;s her opening:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Right under the nose of a president who promised to drain the swamp, one of the government’s shadiest handouts to large banks and big companies looks like it will be renewed for another 25 years. It will not get adequate oversight and congressional review. All it will take is the approval of two out of three U.S. Export-Import Bank directors, who are political appointees.</p>\n<p>That entity is called the Private Export Funding Corporation, or PEFCO.</p>\n<p>I study corporate welfare, and even I was surprised to discover a government entity with such unchecked cronyism and self-dealing. How did it fly under the radar? Despite increasing taxpayers’ exposure to bank debt, it hasn’t received a <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" title=\"\" href=\"https://www.exim.gov/news/chairman-reed-launches-public-review-50-year-exim-private-export-funding-corporation-pefco#:~:text=PEFCO%20entered%20into%20an%20official,%2Dguaranteed%20and%20%2Dinsured%20transactions.\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">public review</a> since it made its <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" title=\"\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/1971/06/23/archives/pefcos-first-major-loan-goes-to-taiwan-power-co.html\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">first loan</a> in 1971.</p></blockquote>\n<p><a href=\"https://techliberation.com/2020/09/03/on-defining-industrial-policy/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Another Mercatus Center colleague, Adam Thierer, writes insightfully about the meaning of the term &#8220;industrial policy.&#8221;</a></p>\n<p><a href=\"https://omny.fm/shows/the-dan-proft-show/donald-j-boudreaux-1\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">I was very pleased to again be a guest on Dan Proft&#8217;s radio program</a>.</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://reason.com/2020/09/02/trumps-eviction-moratorium-could-set-a-dangerous-precedent/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Ilya Somin is right to warn of the great danger lurking in the Trump administration&#8217;s moratorium on evictions</a>. Here&#8217;s the post&#8217;s subheading: &#8220;It&#8217;s a power grab that could undermine federalism and separation of powers, and imperil property rights.&#8221;</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://privpapers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=3668934&#38;dgcid=ejournal_htmlemail_george:mason:university:department:of:economics:research:paper:series_abstractlink\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Here&#8217;s the abstract of an excellent new paper by Chris Coyne, Thomas Duncan, and Abby Hall</a>:</p>\n<blockquote><p>How can public policy best deal with infectious disease? In answering this question, scholarship on the optimal control of infectious disease adopts the model of a benevolent social planner who maximizes social welfare. This approach, which treats the social health planner as a unitary “public health brain” standing outside of society, removes the policymaking process from economic analysis. This paper opens the black box of the social health planner by extending the tools of economics to the policymaking process itself. We explore the nature of the economic problem facing policymakers and the epistemic constraints they face in trying to solve that problem. Additionally, we analyze the incentives facing policymakers in their efforts to address infectious diseases and consider how they affect the design and implementation of public health policy. Finally, we consider how unanticipated system effects emerge due to interventions in complex systems, and how these effects can undermine well-intentioned efforts to improve human welfare. We illustrate the various dynamics of the political economy of state responses to infectious disease by drawing on a range of examples from the COVID-19 pandemic.</p></blockquote>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.aier.org/article/the-covid-19-catastrophe/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Antony Davies and James Harrigan report on some of the costs of the covid lockdowns</a>. A slice:</p>\n<blockquote><p>[P]oliticians invariably feel the need to “do something.” Despite volumes of evidence from disparate fields like economics, social work, ecology, and medicine, it never seems to occur to politicians that sometimes doing less, or even doing nothing, is by far the better approach. Why should it occur to them? When politicians act and their actions do more harm than good, they always say the same thing: “Imagine how bad it would have been had we not acted.”</p>\n<p>But this time, we have evidence. We can compare what happened where politicians reacted with a heavy hand to what happened where they reacted with a light touch. And the evidence we have so far points to the same conclusion: Our politicians destroyed our economy unnecessarily.</p></blockquote>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?a=vfrao0Kwqms:yE-WQ8Z0938:yIl2AUoC8zA\"><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?d=yIl2AUoC8zA\" border=\"0\"></img></a> <a class=\"twitter-share-button\">Tweet</a></p><p>&#8230; is from page 113 of the 2000 Liberty Fund edition of Frederic William Maitland’s profound 1875 dissertation at Trinity College, Cambridge, <em><a href=\"http://www.amazon.com/Historical-Sketch-Liberty-Equality/dp/0865972931/ref=la_B001H6IIX2_1_7?s=books&#38;ie=UTF8&#38;qid=1431689927&#38;sr=1-7\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">A Historical Sketch of Liberty and Equality</a></em>:</p>\n<blockquote><p><i><img class=\"alignleft wp-image-49891\" src=\"https://cafehayek.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/Screen-Shot-2020-09-03-at-1.35.42-PM-300x208.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"212\" height=\"147\" srcset=\"https://cafehayek.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/Screen-Shot-2020-09-03-at-1.35.42-PM-300x208.png 300w, https://cafehayek.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/Screen-Shot-2020-09-03-at-1.35.42-PM.png 424w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 212px) 100vw, 212px\" />If the conventional theory leads to an ideally perfect democracy &#8211; a state in which all that the majority wishes to be law, and nothing else, is law &#8211; then it leads to a form of government under which the arbitrary exercise of power is most certainly possible. Thus, as it progresses, the conventional theory seems to lose its title to be called the doctrine of civil liberty, for it ceases to be a protest against arbitrary forms of restraint.</i></p></blockquote>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?a=WFf0pir8wxk:zRHXBf6beRw:yIl2AUoC8zA\"><img +0000","feedId":13902,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"b411f0ea67bcc9e168d088c9f3cdd837","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Don Boudreaux","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://cafehayek.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/Screen-Shot-2020-09-03-at-1.35.42-PM-300x208.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn71@2020_2/2020/09/05/09-00-01-050_0c69dfd6539b008e.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn30@2020_6/2020/09/05/08-59-57-244_9350ff22b0b602e1.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?d=dnMXMwOfBR0":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn82@2020_2/2020/09/05/09-00-00-776_071e9b731f628c72.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?i=WFf0pir8wxk:zRHXBf6beRw:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn89@2020_3/2020/09/05/09-00-00-261_43d80c90f42a9c7e.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?i=WFf0pir8wxk:zRHXBf6beRw:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn14@2020_5/2020/09/05/09-00-01-137_f69e115eceb5482d.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?i=WFf0pir8wxk:zRHXBf6beRw:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn65@2020_5/2020/09/05/08-59-59-969_5badf8861523cd5f.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/CafeHayek/~4/WFf0pir8wxk":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn62@2020_2/2020/09/05/09-00-00-764_68ecc7c5fa4c7cf3.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1599296397147},{"createdTime":"2020-09-05 16:59:57","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 16:59:57","title":"Material Benefits Aren’t Necessarily ‘Materialistic’","link":"https://cafehayek.com/?p=49871","description":"(Don Boudreaux) \n<!-- Facebook Like Button Vivacity Infotech BEGIN -->\n<div class=\"fb-like\" data-href=\"http://cafehayek.com/2020/09/material-benefits-are-necessarily-materialistic.html\" data-layout=\"standard\" data-action=\"like\" data-show-faces=\"false\" data-size=\"small\" data-width=\"450\" data-share=\"\" ></div>\n<!-- Facebook Like Button Vivacity Infotech END -->\n<p><a href=\"http://twitter.com/share?url=https%3A%2F%2Fcafehayek.com%2F2020%2F09%2Fmaterial-benefits-are-necessarily-materialistic.html&#38;text=Material Benefits Aren't Necessarily 'Materialistic' - Cafe Hayek\" class=\"twitter-share-button\">Tweet</a></p><p><a href=\"https://www.aier.org/article/material-benefits-are-not-merely-material/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">In my latest column for AIER I argue that the non-material benefits made possible by material goods and services are underestimated by Oren Cass</a>. A slice:</p>\n<blockquote><p>A noteworthy instance of this blindness is Cass’s description of the Covid pandemic as causing us Americans to die “in unprecedented numbers.” His claim is untrue. To see why, start by taking the upper end of Virginia Tech professor <a href=\"https://theconversation.com/up-to-204-691-extra-deaths-in-the-us-so-far-in-this-pandemic-year-143139\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Ronald Fricker’s unusually high estimate</a> of the number of excess deaths caused through July 2020 by Covid – specifically, an estimate that Covid has caused the total death rate in the U.S. to be as much as 12 percent higher than otherwise.</p>\n<p>Pre-Covid, <a href=\"https://www.macrotrends.net/countries/USA/united-states/death-rate\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">the United Nations predicted</a> that in 2020 0.888 percent of Americans would die – approximately 2,914,000 persons. Therefore, if Prof. Fricker’s most pessimistic estimate is correct – and if (as almost certainly will <em>not</em> be the case) this estimate holds through January 2021 – Covid will, on an annual basis, raise the number of American deaths by almost 350,000, to a total of roughly 3,264,000. That’s 995 Americans dying per every 100,000. Put differently, on extremely pessimistic assumptions, Covid is on track to raise America’s annual death rate to just shy of 1.0 percent.</p>\n<p>While unquestionably lamentable, this rate of death is hardly unprecedented. The annual rate of death in America did not <em>fall</em> to this level until the late 1940s. One hundred years ago (1920), for example, roughly 1,300 out of every 100,000 Americans died. That’s a death rate of 1.3 percent. (See the graph on page 67 of <a href=\"https://www.cdc.gov/nchs/data/vsus/vsrates1940_60.pdf\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">this study</a> found at the Centers for Disease Control website.)</p>\n<p>Americans’ rate of death fell as we became materially wealthier – as we gained more access not only to medicines such as antibiotics, but also to the detergents, the garbage disposals, the latex gloves, the laptops, and the many other seemingly frivolous goods that global markets routinely pack into the warehouses of Wal-Mart, Target, Home Depot, Safeway, Amazon, and other retailers. We also became better educated and better able to stay connected to family and friends.</p>\n<p>Had this material wealth been produced in less abundance, the range of higher goals that ordinary Americans would today be able to pursue would be narrower. And this material wealth would indeed have been produced in less abundance had Americans been saddled with more of the tariffs, subsidies, and other government interventions for which Oren Cass tirelessly pleads.</p></blockquote>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?a=_el7A3ZriG4:zc2JMYsZAdA:yIl2AUoC8zA\"><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?d=yIl2AUoC8zA\" border=\"0\"></img></a> <a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?a=_el7A3ZriG4:zc2JMYsZAdA:dnMXMwOfBR0\"><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?d=dnMXMwOfBR0\" border=\"0\"></img></a> <a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?a=_el7A3ZriG4:zc2JMYsZAdA:F7zBnMyn0Lo\"><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?i=_el7A3ZriG4:zc2JMYsZAdA:F7zBnMyn0Lo\" border=\"0\"></img></a> <a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?a=_el7A3ZriG4:zc2JMYsZAdA:V_sGLiPBpWU\"><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?i=_el7A3ZriG4:zc2JMYsZAdA:V_sGLiPBpWU\" class=\"twitter-share-button\">Tweet</a></p><p>&#8230; is from John Cochrane’s September 3rd, 2020, blog post, &#8220;<a href=\"https://johnhcochrane.blogspot.com/2020/09/on-looting.html\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">On looting</a>&#8221; (which is a riff on <a href=\"https://www.theatlantic.com/ideas/archive/2020/09/there-no-defense-looting/615925/?fbclid=IwAR1x1iHG9PrANCqoR-qBDnRQS4ylM2ZFB6LBuPq1hJb3oiQmfSKp9W55Ghg\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener src=\"https://cafehayek.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/Unknown-6.jpeg\" alt=\"\" width=\"168\" height=\"168\" srcset=\"https://cafehayek.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/Unknown-6.jpeg 225w, https://cafehayek.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/Unknown-6-150x150.jpeg 150w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 168px) 100vw, 168px\" />Our society and our prosperous economy are built on the bedrock of private property and the rule of law which defends that property. That may not be pretty or fulfill a college sophomore&#8217;s utopia, but the hard lesson of a thousand years is that only private property provides the incentive for people to maintain that property &#8212; farms, houses, factories, stores &#8212; and to put in the immense effort to provide commodities of value to their fellow humans.</em></p></blockquote>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a Boudreaux","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://cafehayek.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/Unknown-6.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn20@2020_4/2020/09/05/09-00-02-612_e1bcf8b25bbf0e39.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn30@2020_6/2020/09/05/08-59-57-244_9350ff22b0b602e1.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?d=dnMXMwOfBR0":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn82@2020_2/2020/09/05/09-00-00-776_071e9b731f628c72.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?i=opHX0MfDnws:AmuEBQZFTWE:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn30@2020_2/2020/09/05/09-00-00-466_2f3d74f5444e6d8b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?i=opHX0MfDnws:AmuEBQZFTWE:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn23@2020_1/2020/09/05/09-00-30-246_8f32435c567d7216.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?i=opHX0MfDnws:AmuEBQZFTWE:gIN9vFwOqvQ":null,"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/CafeHayek/~4/opHX0MfDnws":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn50@2020_5/2020/09/05/09-00-00-571_f8c4fceb4f16643d.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1599296397148},{"createdTime":"2020-09-05 16:59:57","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 16:59:57","title":"‘Expertise’ Seizes Power","link":"https://cafehayek.com/?p=49870","description":"(Don Boudreaux) \n<!-- Facebook Like Button Vivacity Infotech BEGIN -->\n<div class=\"fb-like\" data-href=\"http://cafehayek.com/2020/09/expertise-seizes-power.html\" data-layout=\"standard\" data-action=\"like\" data-show-faces=\"false\" data-size=\"small\" data-width=\"450\" data-share=\"\" ></div>\n<!-- Facebook Like Button Vivacity Infotech END -->\n<p><a href=\"http://twitter.com/share?url=https%3A%2F%2Fcafehayek.com%2F2020%2F09%2Fexpertise-seizes-power.html&#38;text='Expertise' Seizes Power - Cafe Hayek\" class=\"twitter-share-button\">Tweet</a></p><p>From Bill Evers&#8217;s Facebook page I learned of <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2020/08/21/opinion/sunday/giorgio-agamben-philosophy-coronavirus.html?smid=fb-share&#38;fbclid=IwAR3JhVBvI-KX6n95XLRYG7RpW3xh3NwHCL1WC3F5UNuMXr-Bslw_uLfdIrs\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">this recent <i>New York Times</i> op-ed by Christopher Caldwell</a>.</p>\n<p>Caldwell discusses the left-wing Italian philosopher Giorgio Agamben&#8217;s assessment of governments&#8217; reaction to the coronavirus. That I agree more with Agamben&#8217;s take than I do with that of many people whose ideologies and priors are much closer to my own is a sign of these horrible times. (I imagine that Caldwell feels the same way as I do.)</p>\n<p>Here are slices from Caldwell&#8217;s op-ed, but I recommend that it be read in its entirety (emphasis in bold added by me):</p>\n<blockquote><p>This is a common pattern in the Western countries (and American states) where Covid-19 fatalities are dwindling. The arguments for freedom may be strong — but they are put awfully crudely. The arguments for discipline and prevention may often be resented — but they have a lot of scientific authority behind them, and they carry the day. Better safe than sorry. Late last month, Italy’s parliament voted to extend the government’s state of emergency until Oct. 15.</p>\n<p><strong>In a society that respects science, expertise confers power. That has good results, but it brings a terrible problem: Illegitimate political power can be <em>disguised</em> as expertise.</strong> This was a favorite idea of the French philosopher Michel Foucault, who used it to explain how experts had expanded definitions of criminality and sexual deviancy. One of Italy’s most celebrated thinkers, Giorgio Agamben, has recently applied similar insights to the coronavirus, at the risk of turning himself into a national pariah.<br />\n&#8230;..</p>\n<p>His argument about the coronavirus runs along similar lines: The emergency declared by public-health experts replaces the discredited narrative of “national security experts” as a pretext for withdrawing rights and privacy from citizens. “Biosecurity” now serves as a reason for governments to rule in terms of “worst-case scenarios.” This means there is no level of cases or deaths below which locking down an entire nation of 60 million becomes unreasonable. Many European governments, including Italy’s, have developed national contact tracing apps that allow them to track their citizens using cellphones.</p>\n<p>Wars have bequeathed to peacetime a “series of fateful technologies,” Mr. Agamben reminds us, from barbed wire to nuclear power plants. Such innovations tend to be ones that elites were already agitating for, or that align with their interests. Epidemics, he suggests, are no different. He believes that the fateful inheritance of the coronavirus will be social distancing. He is puzzled by the term, “which appeared simultaneously around the world as if it had been prepared in advance.” The expression, he notes, “is not ‘physical’ or ‘personal’ distancing, as would be normal if we were describing a medical measure, but ‘social’ distancing.”</p>\n<p>His point is that social distancing is at least as much a political measure as a public health one, realized so easily because it has been pushed for by powerful forces. Some are straightforward vested interests. Mr. Agamben notes (without naming him) that the former Vodafone chief executive Vittorio Colao, an evangelist for the digitized economy, was put in charge of Italy’s initial transition out of lockdown. Social distancing, Mr. Agamben believes, has also provided Italy’s politicians with a way of hindering spontaneous political organization and stifling the robust intellectual dissent that universities foster.</p>\n<p>The politics of the pandemic expose a deeper ethical, social and even metaphysical erosion. Mr. Agamben cites Italians’ most beloved 19th-century novel, Alessandro Manzoni’s “The Betrothed,” which describes how human relations degenerated in Milan during the plague of 1630. People came to see their neighbors not as fellow human beings but as spreaders of pestilence. As panic set in, authorities executed those suspected of daubing houses with plague germs.</p></blockquote>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://twitter.com/share?url=https%3A%2F%2Fcafehayek.com%2F2020%2F08%2Fto-where-is-americas-middle-class-disappearing.html&#38;text=To Where Is America's Middle-Class 'Disappearing'? - Cafe Hayek\" class=\"twitter-share-button\">Tweet</a></p><p>Here&#8217;s a letter that I sent two weeks ago to the <em>Washington Post</em>:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Editor:</p>\n<p>Kudos to Robert Samuelson for reporting on Stephen Rose’s research on real income growth in America over the past 50 years (“<a href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/opinions/the-rise-of-the-upper-middle-class/2020/08/16/3aa1aea4-de60-11ea-b205-ff838e15a9a6_story.html\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" data-auth=\"NotApplicable\">The rise of the upper middle class</a>,” August 17). But why is news that is unambiguously good – “most of those who have left the middle class have moved up, not down” – buried in the column’s bowels rather than featured as the lede?</p>\n<p>By leading with the finding of rising income inequality, Mr. Samuelson implies that inequality is not only an evil in itself, but one that is more newsworthy than is the fact that most Americans have enjoyed an increase in absolute prosperity.</p>\n<p>Focusing on income differences fuels envy and shrouds what is surely the most important achievement of the American economy in the past half-century: increased prosperity for almost everyone.</p>\n<p>Sincerely,<br />\nDonald J. Boudreaux<br />\nProfessor of Economics<br />\nand<br />\nMartha and Nelson Getchell Chair for the Study of Free Market Capitalism at the Mercatus Center<br />\nGeorge Mason University<br />\nFairfax, VA 22030</p></blockquote>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/CafeHayek?a=b5sttYkww0Y:6iOXtFwOG78:yIl2AUoC8zA\"><img data-width=\"450\" data-share=\"\" ></div>\n<!-- Facebook Like Button Vivacity Infotech END -->\n<p><a href=\"http://twitter.com/share?url=https%3A%2F%2Fcafehayek.com%2F2020%2F08%2Fquotation-of-the-day-3265.html&#38;text=Quotation of the Day... - Cafe Hayek\" class=\"twitter-share-button\">Tweet</a></p><p>&#8230; is from page 39 of Kristian Niemietz’s superb 2019 book, <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/gp/product/B07N7HK6LV/ref=dbs_a_def_rwt_hsch_vapi_tkin_p1_i0\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"><em>Socialism: The Failed Idea That Never Dies</em></a>:</p>\n<blockquote><p><i><img class=\"alignleft wp-image-49838\" src=\"https://cafehayek.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/Screen-Shot-2020-08-27-at-7.02.19-PM-300x48.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"268\" height=\"43\" srcset=\"https://cafehayek.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/Screen-Shot-2020-08-27-at-7.02.19-PM-300x48.png 300w, https://cafehayek.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/Screen-Shot-2020-08-27-at-7.02.19-PM.png 514w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 268px) 100vw, 268px\" />A market economy is a testing ground, in which different business ideas, different management styles, different organisational models and different industry structures can be tried and tested in competition with one another. For example, integrated models, where companies perform a lot of functions in-house, can compete freely with specialised models, where companies outsource many functions to external contractors. In this way, we find out where specialisation is more appropriate, and where integration is more appropriate.</i></p></blockquote>\n<p><strong>DBx</strong>: Yes.</p>\n<p>Each proponent of industrial policy would replace this on-going testing, experimentation, competition, and market-revealed discoveries with the personal fancies and preconceived designs of his or her own puny mind.</p>\n<p>I do not arrogantly criticize the minds of industrial-policy proponents by describing them as &#8220;puny.&#8221; <em>Every</em> human mind &#8211; including, of course, my own &#8211; is puny. No exceptions. The human mind is far too small even to begin to comprehend the amount of knowledge that is routinely discovered and used by competitive market processes.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a to the <em>Wall Street Journal</em>:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Editor</p>\n<p>Jason Riley rightly warns of the dangers lurking in the Progressive assumption that humans are stupid, self-destructive, hate-filled hedonists who can cohere into a productive society only if so regimented by alleged experts – ‘experts’ whose commands each puny individual must obey (“<a href=\"https://www.wsj.com/articles/spare-us-more-of-the-arrogance-of-expertise-11599000757?mod=opinion_lead_pos8\">Spare Us More of the Arrogance of ‘Expertise’</a>,” September 2). Mr. Riley also rightly quotes the great Thomas Sowell in opposition to this arrogant and deeply mistaken notion.</p>\n<p>But my favorite line from Prof. Sowell is a different one. It’s the closing sentence of his 1980 book, <em>Knowledge and Decisions</em>, in which he pleads for “above all, the right of ordinary people to find elbow room for themselves and a refuge from the rampaging presumptions of their ‘betters.’”*</p>\n<p>Sincerely,<br />\nDonald J. Boudreaux<br />\nProfessor of Economics<br />\nand<br />\nMartha and Nelson Getchell Chair for the Study of Free Market Capitalism at the Mercatus Center<br />\nGeorge Mason University<br />\nFairfax, -->\n<div class=\"fb-like\" data-href=\"http://cafehayek.com/2020/08/quotation-of-the-day-3268.html\" data-layout=\"standard\" data-action=\"like\" data-show-faces=\"false\" data-size=\"small\" data-width=\"450\" data-share=\"\" ></div>\n<!-- Facebook Like Button Vivacity Infotech END -->\n<p><a href=\"http://twitter.com/share?url=https%3A%2F%2Fcafehayek.com%2F2020%2F08%2Fquotation-of-the-day-3268.html&#38;text=Quotation of the Day... - Cafe Hayek\" class=\"twitter-share-button\">Tweet</a></p><p>&#8230; is from pages 61-62 of Diane Coyle’s 2014 book, <a href=\"http://www.amazon.com/GDP-Brief-but-Affectionate-History/dp/0691156794/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&#38;ie=UTF8&#38;qid=1392641468&#38;sr=1-1\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"><em>GDP</em></a>:</p>\n<blockquote><p><i><img class=\"alignleft wp-image-49857\" src=\"https://cafehayek.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/images-4.jpeg\" alt=\"\" width=\"249\" height=\"166\" />Nineteen sixty-eight was an iconic year&#8230;. Liberation movements burst into flower around the world. It is not poverty and despair that cause revolutionary activity in modern times, but rather comfortable prosperity. Skipping school to hurl stones at the riot police and evade tear gas is a luxury indulged in only by young people who are not really worried about finding a job when they need one. Likewise, one can afford to experiment with drugs, free love, personal liberation, and self-discovery only in a prosperous economy. By 1968, there had been a quarter century of absolutely extraordinary growth.</i></p></blockquote>\n<p><strong>DBx</strong>: The above insight is important. It does not imply that there are no just causes for protest &#8211; <i>protest</i>; not rioting, looting, and crowd-mad mayhem and disrespect of private property.</p>\n<p>But worthy or not, the ability to protest &#8211; and more generally, the ability to express political opinions &#8211; is enhanced by material prosperity. And so, too (as Coyle notes) does material prosperity make possible other forms of self-expression &#8211; some of which are admirable and others of which are not.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a A slice:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Enter the new paper by Cogan, Heil and Taylor, which finds that, when faced with an inevitable debt explosion in our future, the only viable and desirable option is for Uncle Sam to limit expenditures as a share of GDP, around the 20% ratio that prevailed before the pandemic. The authors find that this approach avoids the &#8220;potentially large increase in future federal taxes.&#8221; Better yet, they actually find that the policy would boost both short- and long-term GDP.</p></blockquote>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.aier.org/article/bidens-passion-and-purpose-is-more-political-paternalism/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Richard Ebeling reminds us that Joe Biden&#8217;s passions are mostly for more power</a>.</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://reason.com/2020/08/26/in-his-rnc-speech-mike-pence-said-joe-biden-will-repeal-trumps-tariffs-if-only-that-were-true/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Eric Boehm reports on how Mike Pence recently, and proudly, displayed his economic ignorance &#8211; sadly an ignorance shared by Joe Biden</a>.</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.econlib.org/good-news-from-georgia/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Here&#8217;s David Henderson on the <em>Wall Street Journal</em> on the state of Georgia&#8217;s response to covid.</a></p>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.cato.org/blog/rights-women\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">David Boaz writes about women&#8217;s rights</a>.</p>\n<p><a href=\"http://www.jeffjacoby.com/24500/honey-they-shrunk-the-gop-platform\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Jeff Jacoby is understandably disturbed by the Trumpification of the Republican party</a>. Here&#8217;s his conclusion:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Unquestioning support for the supreme leader is not a democratic value, as Republicans emphasized just four years ago. In its 2016 platform, the Republican National Committee blasted Barack Obama and the Democrats for having &#8220;changed what John Adams called &#8216;a government of laws and not of men&#8217; into just the opposite.&#8221; This year there is no platform.
